    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<div id="descriptionContainer">

<span id="freeTextContainer4614408188743083022">The story follows Prince Prospero's attempts to avoid a dangerous plague known as the Red Death by hiding in his abbey. He, along with many other wealthy nobles, has a masquerade ball within seven rooms of his abbey, each decorated with a different color.<br/><br/> In the midst of their revelry, a mysterious figure disguised as a Red Death victim enters and makes his way through eac</span>
  <span id="freeText4614408188743083022" style="display:none">The story follows Prince Prospero's attempts to avoid a dangerous plague known as the Red Death by hiding in his abbey. He, along with many other wealthy nobles, has a masquerade ball within seven rooms of his abbey, each decorated with a different color.<br /><br /> In the midst of their revelry, a mysterious figure disguised as a Red Death victim enters and makes his way through each of the rooms. <br /><br /> The story follows many traditions of Gothic fiction and is often analyzed as an allegory about the inevitability of death, though some critics advise against an allegorical reading. Many different interpretations have been presented, as well as attempts to identify the true nature of the titular disease.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4614408188743083022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_849933927" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/849933927" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/849933927?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer849933927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1568495254602348285"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1494284558i/22700900._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I’ve always sensed a strong connection to Poe’s <i>The Masque of the Red Death</i>, perhaps because I've both played and listen to loads of medieval music, perhaps because I enjoy the art and history and philosophy of that period, or, perhaps because I’ve always been drawn to literature dealing with issues of life and death. Whatever the reason, I love this tale. Here are my reflections on several themes: <br/><br/>THE REALITY<br/>The tale’s Red Death sounds like the Black Death of 1349 where a family member could be</span>
  <span id="freeText1568495254602348285" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1494284558i/22700900._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I’ve always sensed a strong connection to Poe’s <i>The Masque of the Red Death</i>, perhaps because I've both played and listen to loads of medieval music, perhaps because I enjoy the art and history and philosophy of that period, or, perhaps because I’ve always been drawn to literature dealing with issues of life and death. Whatever the reason, I love this tale. Here are my reflections on several themes: <br><br>THE REALITY<br>The tale’s Red Death sounds like the Black Death of 1349 where a family member could be perfectly healthy in the morning, start feeling sick at noon, spit blood and be in excruciating pain in the evening and be dead by midnight. It was that quick. Living at the time of the Black Death, one Italian chronicler wrote, “They died by the hundreds, both day and night, and all were thrown in ... ditches and covered with earth. And as soon as those ditches were filled, more were dug. And I, Agnolo di Tura ... buried my five children with my own hands ... And so many died that all believed it was the end of the world.”<br> <br>THE DENIAL<br>Let the Red Death take those on the outside. Prince Prospero took steps to make sure his castle would be a sanctuary, a secure refuge where, once bolted inside, amid a carefully constructed world of festival, a thousand choice friends could revel in merriment with jugglers, musicians, dancers and an unlimited supply of wine. And then, “It was toward the close of the fifth or sixth month of his seclusion, and while the pestilence raged most furiously abroad, that the Prince Prospero entertained his thousand friends at a masked ball of the most unusual magnificence. It was a voluptuous scene, that masquerade.” Classic Edgar Allan Poe foreshadowing.<br><br>THE NUMBER SEVEN<br>The prince constructed seven rooms for his revelers. And there is all that medieval symbolism for the number seven, such as seven gifts of the holy spirit, Seven Seals from the Book of Revelation, seven liberal arts, the seven virtues and, of course, the seven deadly sins (gluttony, lechery, avarice, luxury, wrath, envy, and sloth), which sounds like a catalogue of activities within the castle walls.<br><br>THE SEVENTH ROOM - THE BLACK CHAMBER<br>Keeping in mind the medieval symbolism for the color black with associations of darkness, evil, the devil, power and secrecy, we read, “But in the western or black chamber the effect of the fire light that streamed upon the dark hangings through the blood-tinted panes, was ghastly in the extreme, and produced so wild a look upon the countenances of those who entered, that there were few of the company bold enough to set foot within its precincts at all.” We are told the prince’s plans were bold and fiery and barbaric, but, as we read the tale, we see how even a powerful prince can be outflanked by the fiery and chaotic side of life itself. <br><br>THE CLOCK<br>This seventh chamber has a huge ebony clanging clock. A reminder for both eye and ear that the prince can supply his revelers and himself with an unlimited supply of wine but there is one thing he doesn’t have the power to provide – an unlimited amount of time.<br><br>THE UNEXPECTED MASKER<br>When the clock clangs twelve times, a tall, gaunt, blood-spotted, corpse-like reveler appears in the black chamber. Poe, master storyteller that he is, pens one of my all-time favorite lines: “Even with the utterly lost, to whom life and death are equally jests, there are matters of which no jest can be made.” Not a lot of merriment once the revelers start dropping like blood-covered, despairing flies. <br><br>THE PSYCHOLOGICAL TALE<br>We read how there are some who think the prince mad. After all, what is a Poe tale without the possibility of madness? Additionally, when the revelers attempt to seize the intruder with his grey garments and corpse-like mask, they come away with nothing. If these revelers were minutes from an agonizing plague-induced death, how sharp are their senses, really? To what extent is their experience the play of the mind?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1494284558i/22700901.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="1568495254602348285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2030328610" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2030328610" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65909643-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515603070p2/65909643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2030328610?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/65909643-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65909643-michael?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2030328610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5179179141787661626">Fascinating and lurid allegory about a group of people who, on the invitation of "Prince Prospero," lock themselves within a "castellated abbey" to escape the Red Death. The inhabitants of the abbey are provided "all the appliances of pleasure," and boy do they know how to party: "there were buffoons, there were improvisatori, there were ballet-dancers, there were musicians, there was Beauty, there was wine." It all culminates in a huge masked ball held in several colorful and gaudy chambers: "T</span>
  <span id="freeText5179179141787661626" style="display:none">Fascinating and lurid allegory about a group of people who, on the invitation of "Prince Prospero," lock themselves within a "castellated abbey" to escape the Red Death. The inhabitants of the abbey are provided "all the appliances of pleasure," and boy do they know how to party: "there were buffoons, there were improvisatori, there were ballet-dancers, there were musicians, there was Beauty, there was wine." It all culminates in a huge masked ball held in several colorful and gaudy chambers: "There was much glare and glitter and piquancy and phantasm.... There was much of the beautiful, much of the wanton, much of the bizarre, something of the terrible, and not a little of that which might have excited disgust." Then who shows up, of course, but a figure dressed as a Red Death victim: "His vesture was dabbled in blood--and his broad brow, with all the features of the face, was besprinkled with the scarlet horror." Prince Prospero becomes seriously pissed-off at this figure because he's spoiling all the fun, everyone is scared and freaked out, but when he confronts him he sees that there's literally nothing behind the mask, and he drops dead, and soon everyone else does too. <br /><br />So what is Poe saying here? (I find myself searching for the answer to this question because of the allegorical nature of the work itself.) For one thing, that you can't cheat death, but I think there's something more profound going on, a sort of sociological take on how people ignore the suffering of others at their peril. That we can't really wall ourselves off and party in the face of others' suffering because that suffering will inevitably reach us too. We can't ignore others' pain or pretend it doesn't exist or look the other way.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5179179141787661626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13146341162674583702" style="display:none">Kurt Vonnegut, Ray Bradbury, HP Lovecraft and Edgar Allan Poe are playing a round of golf in the Great Hereafter and discussing Poe’s short work The Masque of the Red Death.<br><br>Vonnegut: Damn! Hooked it.<br><br>Lovecraft: You’ve been pulling it left all day, you raised your head on the swing.<br><br>Bradbury: I saw you move your front foot.<br><br>Poe: You need to keep your arm straighter.<br><br>Vonnegut: OK! Damn it. Ed, what in the hell made you write the Red Death story?<br><br>Bradbury: Masque of the Red Death, one of my favorites, this influenced me in so many ways.<br><br>Lovecraft: Me too, the idea of a surreptitious plague being intentionally shared with the well to do was too good.<br><br>Poe: I think I was struck by the historic discrepancy between the haves and the have nots in material wealth and position and yet death makes no such distinctions.<br><br>Bradbury: You smacked the hell out of the ball HP, was that your 3 wood?<br><br>Lovecraft: 4 wood, I know! I’m getting at least par, if I don’t choke on the green. Ed, were you making a class distinction?<br><br>Poe: Nice shot HP, well yes and no. Certainly the setting of the masquerade party while the rest of the city was suffering and dying was a statement about class differences and especially with the insensitivity of the aristocracy but more than that, I wanted to convey a sense of poetic justice.<br><br>Vonnegut: Damn it! I can’t buy a straight shot today!<br><br>Bradbury: You raised your head again.<br><br>Lovecraft: You jerked your backswing.<br><br>Poe: It might help if you would put out the cigarette.<br><br>Vonnegut: Thanks [lighting another] and you made a very early observation about airborne pathogens, this was published in the 1840s right?<br><br>Poe: 1842, right, but honestly the infection was more of a symbolic rather than a medical set up. Wow! Nice chip HP.<br><br>Bradbury: Yeah, wow, you’re shooting for a birdie, right?<br><br>Lovecraft: Yeah! So Ed, what about the masquerade party? Was this just a framing device to allow the Red Death carrier to visit the party?<br><br>Poe: Well, yes, but also I think I was trying to create a metaphor for the masks that we wear in society, figuratively speaking that would allow these partyers to ignore the misery of their neighbors. And remember, you twentieth century guys are far more removed from true detached aristocracy, back in the day, if you weren’t part of the in crowd, your life or death mattered very little. Whoa! Great putt HP!<br><br>Lovecraft: Thanks Ed. Kurt, looks like you’re buying the beer.<br><br>Vincent Price [from another hole] FOUR!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1566252912i/28017231.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_1394462229" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1394462229" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1394462229?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=things-go-bump-in-the-night">things-go-bump-in-the-night</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=the-shorts">the-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1394462229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16150342695258804859"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442593384i/16256508._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='175' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>I don't know how I overlooked "The Masque of the Red Death" when I was going through my Poe phase a while back, but someone's review reminded me of it (Thanks, random Goodreads friend!). So I found a copy of it online <a href='http://xroads.virginia.edu/~hyper/POE/masque.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> and gave it another read to refresh my memory. <br/><br/>This story is both less and more than I recalled. It's long on setting and mood and short on plot. The plot could probably be described in about two sentences. Let's give it a try:<blockquote>A deadly plague is ravaging the land, and the unf</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText16150342695258804859" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442593384i/16256508._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="175" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I don't know how I overlooked "The Masque of the Red Death" when I was going through my Poe phase a while back, but someone's review reminded me of it (Thanks, random Goodreads friend!). So I found a copy of it online <a href="http://xroads.virginia.edu/~hyper/POE/masque.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> and gave it another read to refresh my memory. <br><br>This story is both less and more than I recalled. It's long on setting and mood and short on plot. The plot could probably be described in about two sentences. Let's give it a try:<blockquote>A deadly plague is ravaging the land, and the unfeeling Prince shuts himself up in his castle with about a thousand of his partying friends.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[But Death, personified, crashes the party and everybody dies. The end. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</blockquote>But the setting - whew! If you like creepy Gothic and grotesque Baroque, you really need to give this a read. The seven rooms, with their different color schemes and the disturbing black and red room at the end, the strange ebony clock, the bizarre masqueraders...<br><br>Random thoughts gleaned from surfing the web:<br><br>☠ The Red Death plague is not an actual disease, though Poe may have been thinking of the Black Death, or tuberculosis, or cholera, or some amalgamation of these or other actual diseases.<br><br>☠ "Masque" could be short for the partiers' masquerade ball, or an alternative spelling of "mask," recalling the mask worn by the Red Death. In the story's initial publication the title was actually spelled "The Mask of the Red Death." But "masque" is also defined as a "short allegorical dramatic entertainment." That's a fascinating description of what the Red Death is doing at the end of the story!<br><br>☠ There's an interesting Minecraft image of what the seven rooms may have looked like:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442593117i/16256486.jpg" width="300" height="230" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>☠ There are also some interesting theories about the seven rooms representing the seven stages of life, with birth (blue) at the beginning and death (red and black) at the end:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442593117i/16256487._SX540_.png" width="360" height="225" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>Though personally I feel like most of the colors and their order are a bit random for this theory. However, I think the mystical symbolism of the number seven does play into Poe's use of it in this story.<br><br>Poe stated that he disliked didactic or preachy stories. But in spite of this dislike, I believe he created an allegorical story here with a strong moral message.<br><br><i>Art credits:</i><br>- Still from 1964 film <i>The Masque of the Red Death</i>, starring Vincent Price.<br>- The Minecraft art is from a YouTube video: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oPyOk3JpMTY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oPyOk...</a><br>- I found the seven stages of life pic on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://mohamadshahine.blogspot.com/2015/02/themasque-of-red-death-is-short-story.html">http://mohamadshahine.blogspot.com/20...</a>, but I'm not sure who the original artist is.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="16150342695258804859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1476038647" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1476038647" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1476038647?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21269086-the-masque-of-the-red-death">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=darkness-horror-gothic">darkness-horror-gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1476038647" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2745431248231698849">Death waits for no man. Time can’t stop the inevitable; it can only delay it. This tale, perhaps, embodies the idea that death is an unavoidable end for all; it is one that we all must embrace because it simply cannot be escaped. Time will run out for everyone eventually. <br/><br/><p>
  <i>And now was acknowledged the presence of Red Death. He had come like a thief in the night. And one by one dropped the revellers in the blood-bedewed halls of their revel, and died each in his despairing posture of his fall. </i></p></span>
  <span id="freeText2745431248231698849" style="display:none">Death waits for no man. Time can’t stop the inevitable; it can only delay it. This tale, perhaps, embodies the idea that death is an unavoidable end for all; it is one that we all must embrace because it simply cannot be escaped. Time will run out for everyone eventually. <br><br><p>
  <i>And now was acknowledged the presence of Red Death. He had come like a thief in the night. And one by one dropped the revellers in the blood-bedewed halls of their revel, and died each in his despairing posture of his fall. And the life of the ebony clock went out with that of the last of the gay. And the flames of the tripods expired. And Darkness and Decay and the Red Death held illimitable dominion over all. </i>
</p><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450791361i/17469972._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In this story Poe is not blatant. He is obscure, subtle and a little bit profound. There are several layers of meaning in here, which can each produce a new interpretation. Indeed, in order to escape the approaching “Red Death” Prince Prospero decides to hide in a series of abbeys. They shield out the approaching darkness, and to accompany him he takes one thousand knights and gentlemen; he only takes the so called best of what society has to offer. Together they wait out the blight that infests their land. They party and they frolic; they relax and they become complacent. Well, until a mysterious entity turns up and murders them all. <br><br>It’s shapeless and spectral; it wears a red party mask and almost blends in with the gathered sycophants. This really got me thinking. What exactly is this “red death?” The ending is suggestive of a bloody death for all those gathered, but the beginning speaks a different tale. It is suggestive of a blunting of emotion and a separation from the infected fellow man. It speaks of an incoming petulance. Either way the fate remains the same for all. No man, whether he is high or low born, can escape death. Poe’s allegory hints that those who attempt to avoid the inevitable will, ultimately, be punished. That would explain why the party guests receive the most gruesome of endings. They received a real blood “red death” rather than a common passing. <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450791297i/17469967._SX540_.png" width="400" height="100" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="2745431248231698849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_759269401" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/759269401" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/759269401?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-m-p">read-authors-m-p</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=ghost-horror-supernatural">ghost-horror-supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer759269401" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10775920759785666604"><b>The Masque of the Red Death</b>, written in 1842 by Edgar Allan Poe, is a surprisingly short story, which is generally regarded to be allegorical. In it, Prince Prospero is so terrified of the pestilential <i>"Red Death"</i>, that he walls himself and a thousand wealthy nobles up in his castellated abbey, where they have a masquerade ball, moving from room to room. Obviously they are going to come to a sticky end. At the time of writing Poe's wife was suffering from tuberculosis, and there was an epidemic </span>
  <span id="freeText10775920759785666604" style="display:none"><b>The Masque of the Red Death</b>, written in 1842 by Edgar Allan Poe, is a surprisingly short story, which is generally regarded to be allegorical. In it, Prince Prospero is so terrified of the pestilential <i>"Red Death"</i>, that he walls himself and a thousand wealthy nobles up in his castellated abbey, where they have a masquerade ball, moving from room to room. Obviously they are going to come to a sticky end. At the time of writing Poe's wife was suffering from tuberculosis, and there was an epidemic of cholera in Baltimore which he saw, so it is likely that he was very preoccupied with illness and death at the time.<br /><br />Nevertheless this is a beautifully painted story. The seven-chambered apartment is vividly described, each having its own colour both by furnishings and illuminated by coloured light through the windows. A sense of foreboding is created as the final room is black, with blood-red light. There is much festivity as the guests move through the chambers, until <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[they come across a shrouded figure in a blood-spattered robe, whose mask looks like the face of a cadaver. Prospero chases the figure to the end room, but dies horribly after confronting it, as do all the guests. At the end of the story it becomes evident that the intangible spectre was the <i>"Red Death"</i> itself. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> The author has used one of his favourite settings, a castle; the large clock clanging every hour increases the mounting tension; <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the spectre at the end causing the demise of all the characters in a gruesome unearthly way; <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> all these are classic Poe.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10775920759785666604" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3609468347" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3609468347" />
    <a title="Meike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59050228-meike"><img alt="Meike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514995700p2/59050228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3609468347?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meike" href="/user/show/59050228-meike">Meike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/53669376-the-masque-of-the-red-death">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3609468347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14736980803336289844">Imagine there is a mysterious illnes ravaging the country and a rich leader decides to deny reality by partying with his croonies in a well-protected mansion - no, Poe did not somehow foresee the sad tale of Prince Stupido in the White House, but it's unsettling how timely his "Masque of the Red Death" currently is. In the classic short story, Prince Prospero (he's like, prosperous, get it?) witnesses half of his population wasting away due to a plague that leads to profuse bleeding and death wi</span>
  <span id="freeText14736980803336289844" style="display:none">Imagine there is a mysterious illnes ravaging the country and a rich leader decides to deny reality by partying with his croonies in a well-protected mansion - no, Poe did not somehow foresee the sad tale of Prince Stupido in the White House, but it's unsettling how timely his "Masque of the Red Death" currently is. In the classic short story, Prince Prospero (he's like, prosperous, get it?) witnesses half of his population wasting away due to a plague that leads to profuse bleeding and death within 30 minutes after contraction, and because that's kinda distressing, he summons 1000 of his noble buddies and hides in an abbey. But of course, there is no contemplation: "The prince had provided all the appliances of pleasure. There were buffoons, there were improvisatori, there were ballet-dancers, there were musicians, there was Beauty, there was wine. All these and security were within. Without was the Red Death." After 5 or 6 months, they throw a party feat. Venetian masks and all in the seven colorfully designed rooms - and guess who's coming to join the extravaganza? Yep, the Red Death.<br /><br />It's possible to read this as a morality tale about the inevitability of death, the great equalizer, but that would be atypical for Poe - it would be a mistake to see it solely as that. Poe always writes about the functions of the human mind, and it's interesting how he investigates our longing for escapism. Seven rooms, time approaching seven months - seven deadly sins, and while Prospero and his buddies indulge in some of them, they are also victims of the human condition as such, a.k.a. the original sin. And then there is the maze-like architecture of the abbey, where windows are described like eyes (hello, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175516.The_Fall_of_the_House_of_Usher" title="The Fall of the House of Usher by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fall of the House of Usher</a>), and the final black room is decorated like a coffin and contains an ebony clock counting our earthly time. The house, it also reflects the intricate structure and colorful moods and emotions of the human mind.<br /><br />So what's the condition that's killing people off, who is the Red Death? The masks immediately make you think of the plague doctor, but the guests of the masquerade are just imposters trying to heal by denying the condition. Considering the fact that Poe's mother, foster mother and brother died of tuberculosis and his wife/cousin Virgina was suffering from it while he wrote the story, this might be what he had in mind. The illness could also refer to cholera, as the author witnessed a cholera epidemic in Baltimore. In a non literal sense, it could simply stand for sin, or the cruelty of random destiny that you can't escape.<br /><br />Do I really have to point out that everybody needs to read Poe, because he was a damn genius? His stories show the potential of gothic horror as high literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14736980803336289844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7492721711643610204">The timeless lesson taught to us through Edgar Allan Poe's 'The Masque of the Red Death' is: don't do any partying when there is a fucking virus outbreak going on!</span>

<div id="review_524947226" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/524947226" />
    <a title="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder"><img alt="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572373103p2/3048260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/524947226?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" href="/user/show/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder">Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer524947226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11448166947393047669">In one of my Literature textbooks, this is the story the book chose to best set the example of how important setting can be to a story.<br/><br/>Poe's incredible talent in setting mood through the most miniscule of details is powerful as he establishes dread, irony, and a hefty infusion of Gothic feel by detailing the colors of a series of rooms and what they represent to the audience and characters. The symbolism of the clock is musical and alluring; the ominous clang and the dancers reactions, with its </span>
  <span id="freeText11448166947393047669" style="display:none">In one of my Literature textbooks, this is the story the book chose to best set the example of how important setting can be to a story.<br /><br />Poe's incredible talent in setting mood through the most miniscule of details is powerful as he establishes dread, irony, and a hefty infusion of Gothic feel by detailing the colors of a series of rooms and what they represent to the audience and characters. The symbolism of the clock is musical and alluring; the ominous clang and the dancers reactions, with its dong indicating the time, further spells out a foreboding mood and tone.<br /><br />Even the pattern the rooms are walked through speaks volumes. The first room as light blue can symbolize brightness and innocence, skies and springs and births and new beginnings. Each of the seven rooms has a window, all with the color matching the interior of their walls, the exception being the final, seventh room: black.<br /><br />Poe has stated that stories are best enjoyed if they can be read in one sitting. The Masque of the Red Death is indeed short, only a few pages long, and so it should speak volumes that Poe chose this short space to go into detail about the rooms. He goes into the most detail about the black, final room as its significance - death, the ultimate end, the irony - is the most important element of the story. It is also in this room that the clock beckons and waits.<br /><br />Without getting into details about any of the characters, Poe concentrates on setting and the most important and only qualities about the prince that the audience needs to know - his fear of the Red Plague and death, his ultimate arrogance in the face of death, believing he can seal it off and defeat it by abiding within his castle walls.<br /><br />The party-goers feel the same, reassured by the self-imposed power the prince claims, dancing around at midnight behind their masks, stopping only when the clock chimes its ominous call, feeling a small hesitation but quickly ignoring it again as they resume merry dancing and happily embracing false securities. Death as the ultimate, inevitable force erupts onto the party. The prince then proceeds from room to room in a circular order, indicating from life to different stages of color, to the inevitable black which is the end room, from which there is no escape.<br /><br />Poe was an original type of writer who aspired to make a solid career as a literary critic. Confident in his writing ability and seeking to inject freshness into words by developing the world's first detective story and gothic pieces which whispered doses of irony, he isn't the type to resort to already used phrases or cliches. Because of this, I find high relevance in the ending paragraph, where he writes:<br /><br /><b>And now was acknowledged the presence of the Red Death. He had come like a thief in the night.</b><br /><br />Instantly I recognized "come like a thief in the night" as the biblical words spoken by Jesus when referring to the apocalypse. It would come without warning and begin the reign of death, as He comes "like a thief in the night."<br /><br />A powerful tale about the finality of an ending which can't be avoided, Poe is to be admired for capturing such a significant range of emotions using creative settings in a short span of pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11448166947393047669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2835572713" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3738618730952934595">This is a typical type Poe short story in terms of its dark, gloomy, gothic atmosphere and also its obscurity since the story requires deep-analysis and interpretation. However, it is different from others due to its didactic message: Death is inevitable, no matter what you do and no matter who you are, you cannot escape it just like the Prince Prospero - an allusion to the Tempest. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2597409991" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2597409991" />
    <a title="Shelly Strange" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25393430-shelly-strange"><img alt="Shelly Strange" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600285315p2/25393430.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2597409991?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shelly Strange" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shelly Strange" href="/user/show/25393430-shelly-strange">Shelly Strange</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25393430-shelly-strange?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25393430-shelly-strange?shelf=horror">horror</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2597409991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14264554768411766244">I liked this one a lot more than <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>. The Red Death is a fictional plague that may or may not have been based on consumption/tuberculosis. Prince Prospero is a dreadful man who is vulgar with his wealth while his country suffers around him. He hosts a huge ball and of course, meets a grisly demise.<br/><br/>A lot of time was spent, it seemed, on describing the various rooms, which each had a color and theme. The revelers were all too happy to join him in celebration until things start to g</span>
  <span id="freeText14264554768411766244" style="display:none">I liked this one a lot more than <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>. The Red Death is a fictional plague that may or may not have been based on consumption/tuberculosis. Prince Prospero is a dreadful man who is vulgar with his wealth while his country suffers around him. He hosts a huge ball and of course, meets a grisly demise.<br /><br />A lot of time was spent, it seemed, on describing the various rooms, which each had a color and theme. The revelers were all too happy to join him in celebration until things start to go south.<br /><br />This is a very short story and easy to read. Poe likes long paragraphs. There were several sentences/quotes I really liked, like this one:<br /><br /><i>There are chords in the hearts of the most reckless which cannot be touched without emotion.</i><br /><br />This one is spooky and dark and really goth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14264554768411766244" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3367531562" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3367531562" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3367531562?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=short-stories-novellas">short-stories-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3367531562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12126730759443831860">Edgar Allan Poe’s highly symbolic short story about death and the futility of attempting to avoid it, is one of the most read short stories of all time. In these days of pestilence abroad, it made for a reminder that things could be worse, but also that time marches regardless of circumstance and that death is to come for all of us, and often when least expected.<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41884037-kimberly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41884037-kimberly?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41884037-kimberly?shelf=gothic-literature">gothic-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2965908012" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13063239840975276924">When I think of Gothic literature, Edgar Allen Poe is sure to come to mind. Although it is a short story, the Masque of the Red Death is chilling. Poe sets up a very atmospheric read with the colorful and indulgent Prince Prospero and the mysterious guest who is both frightening and foreboding. I think the Masque of the Red Death is a fine example of why Poe's works are considered classics.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1263945624516321979" style="display:none">Re-Read, 11/5/17: <br />Maybe my favorite Poe. He has this style, that makes you feel this rush and think, dang, that's cool! This time, I don't know if perspectives changed or if I understood the objectivity of the piece better, but I felt a visceral terror flowing through my body from my gut that ended in an explosion of thrill and high, and bodily reactions of goosebumps and chill-bumps in the end. This Red Death guy - not someone I'd like to meet at a gathering. He wears a mask of a human face contorted in agony of plague-death, with wet, shining blood splish-splashed across his death garments. The feeling resembles that of a super-zombie, but one of extreme intelligence and god-likeness. One by one, they all fall down, before the Masque of The Red Death. <br /><br />Poe, Poe, Poe. Amazing! His prose sinks into your mind, setting the soul on a fire of fear. Man, the personification of a plague, stylized Zorro-like. Disturbing!</span>

<div id="review_1424081780" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1424081780" />
    <a title="Mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23106940-mia"><img alt="Mia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600049623p2/23106940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1424081780?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mia" href="/user/show/23106940-mia">Mia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=dark-grim-scary">dark-grim-scary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1424081780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11225278526055820791">I love the premise- fearing a horrible sickness that has seized his lands, a hedonistic prince locks himself and hundreds of his friends away in a castle, with an enormous wall running the length of it so nothing can get in or out. But soon the prince's fanciful denial is shattered in a very... strange way.<br/><br/>Basically, what my dislike of this comes down to is the length. It's only four pages, which (for me at least) simply is not enough time to become invested in a story and care about its ending.</span>
  <span id="freeText11225278526055820791" style="display:none">I love the premise- fearing a horrible sickness that has seized his lands, a hedonistic prince locks himself and hundreds of his friends away in a castle, with an enormous wall running the length of it so nothing can get in or out. But soon the prince's fanciful denial is shattered in a very... strange way.<br /><br />Basically, what my dislike of this comes down to is the length. It's only four pages, which (for me at least) simply is not enough time to become invested in a story and care about its ending. The writing is beautiful as always, but there was something <i>missing</i>, something I couldn't quite put my finger on. I didn't really care about the plot, wasn't even rooting for the selfish Prince Prospero to get his comeuppance.<br /><br />The idea of the rooms of all different colours just kinda made me shrug, the same way I shrugged in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5297.The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="The Picture of Dorian Gray by Oscar Wilde" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Picture of Dorian Gray</a> when everybody is avoiding Dorian because of his incredibly scandalous lifestyle- which pretty much entails collecting foreign instruments and fancy books. I feel that both of those things were supposed to have a greater affect on the reader than they did. Perhaps I'm missing the symbolism of the rooms, though I do have my own theory about the castle itself: that it <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[represents the human mind, or maybe just the mind of Prospero, and as much as the enormous ballroom tries, it will never be able to shut out what comes from the black room, which I took to represent paranoia and the knowledge of impending death. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Again, I feel I must impress that this may be completely and totally wrong, and maybe my impeded grasp of symbolism is what kept me from enjoying this short tale. It is what it is.<br /><br />I much preferred <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/419520.Ligeia" title="Ligeia by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener"> Ligeia</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175516.The_Fall_of_the_House_of_Usher" title="The Fall of the House of Usher by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Fall of the House of Usher</a>, which, though they're not as well-known as The Masque of the Red Death, are longer and more fleshed-out. Read it <a href="http://xroads.virginia.edu/~hyper/POE/masque.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here.</a><br /><br /><br />(Though the Month of Poe is still in full swing, once Halloween passes I'm going to take a little break from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/73449.Great_Tales_and_Poems" title="Great Tales and Poems by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener"> Great Tales and Poems</a>. I'm in the mood for some action-adventure fantasy and was thinking of trying <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68428.The_Final_Empire__Mistborn___1_" title="The Final Empire (Mistborn, #1) by Brandon Sanderson" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Final Empire</a>, unless any of you guys have some recommendations for a good fantasy book? I haven't explored the genre very much, and I think it's high time I do.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11225278526055820791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2412671228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2412671228" />
    <a title="Rachel Aranda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43622386-rachel-aranda"><img alt="Rachel Aranda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585436207p2/43622386.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2412671228?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Aranda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Aranda" href="/user/show/43622386-rachel-aranda">Rachel Aranda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18998293-the-masque-of-the-red-death">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43622386-rachel-aranda?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43622386-rachel-aranda?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2412671228&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2412671228" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4772707647475521889">I first read "The Masque of the Red Death" when I was a junior in high school. I remember liking it but it wasn't my favorite Poe short story that I’d read at the time. I decided to reread this book when one of my friends (shoutout to Deanna) read it recently and decided it was time to see if my opinion had changed at all. One reason I was interested in reading this story was that I did like Poe's other stories at the time, and this one was always so well received for its symbolism and reference</span>
  <span id="freeText4772707647475521889" style="display:none">I first read "The Masque of the Red Death" when I was a junior in high school. I remember liking it but it wasn't my favorite Poe short story that I’d read at the time. I decided to reread this book when one of my friends (shoutout to Deanna) read it recently and decided it was time to see if my opinion had changed at all. One reason I was interested in reading this story was that I did like Poe's other stories at the time, and this one was always so well received for its symbolism and references in "The Shining." It seems waiting a decade to reread this short story hasn’t altered my views on liking it any.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The story talks about the Prince Prospero and other nobles, who escape a plague (Red Death) and settle down in Prospero’s abbey. At a masquerade ball party, Prospero and the other nobles enjoy their time. At midnight, a mysterious dark figure appears, as it has the same traits, which the Red Death per se has. Prospero asks everyone around to identify this figure’s identity since it wears a mask. Prospero decides to hang whomever is behind this mask. The nobles do not approach it. Prospero trails the figure as he draws a dagger. However, the figure vanquishes Prospero, who falls dead. The mask is fallen—nothing is behind it—but the nobles realise that whatever behind it, is the Red Death itself. They all fall dead because of it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The story itself, and the ending are really well written, compared to some of the other dark Poe endings in quality. To me, the sagging middle that pulls me right out of really enjoying this story as much as I could have his other work. I get so distracted by the description here to actually enjoy the story. I think there might be more description than story. Normally I find the descriptions in Poe's work to be a huge plus, but it's honestly this particular story's biggest detriment.<br /><br />I would still recommend reading this, as none of Poe's works should be missed out on. I know most people do enjoy this one and it is one of his unknown works among “youth” today, but once you read it you usually don’t forget it. The actual story and the ending are still of excellent quality despite my complaints.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4772707647475521889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3952838945414172487">I downloaded a free public domain ebook of The Masque of the Red Death short story for a spooky October read. <br/><br/>I've read this in one sitting; it's very short. It's also very topical since <b>it's set during an epidemic, and the characters were in quarantine together</b>. So I took the story literally, even though some critics interpret it allegorically. I liked the writing. <br/><br/>I've read some of Poe's other short stories (including horror) in middle school and liked them at the time. But that was over 10 ye</span>
  <span id="freeText3952838945414172487" style="display:none">I downloaded a free public domain ebook of The Masque of the Red Death short story for a spooky October read. <br /><br />I've read this in one sitting; it's very short. It's also very topical since <b>it's set during an epidemic, and the characters were in quarantine together</b>. So I took the story literally, even though some critics interpret it allegorically. I liked the writing. <br /><br />I've read some of Poe's other short stories (including horror) in middle school and liked them at the time. But that was over 10 years ago. Not sure if I'll read more of his short stories now. I like Poe's writing, but I'm generally not into short stories. I own a volume of his selected poetry though, so I'll definitely read that! </span>

<div id="review_1525810425" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1525810425" />
    <a title="Anusha Narasimhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16375752-anusha-narasimhan"><img alt="Anusha Narasimhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468941834p2/16375752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1525810425?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anusha Narasimhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anusha Narasimhan" href="/user/show/16375752-anusha-narasimhan">Anusha Narasimhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16375752-anusha-narasimhan?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1525810425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5270043755865663985">I guess the message is to embrace social distancing and not throw parties during epidemics/pandemics. Also, you can't assume that you are somehow immune and disregard an infectious disease as "other people's problem" because the Red Death (or the coronavirus) does not discriminate.<br/><br/>Perhaps if Poe wrote this story today, there may be some hint at washing hands regularly, not hoarding hand sanitizers and not fighting over toilet paper.<br/><br/><b>Edit:</b><br/>Bumping up the rating to 5 stars in light of recent events.</span>
  <span id="freeText5270043755865663985" style="display:none">I guess the message is to embrace social distancing and not throw parties during epidemics/pandemics. Also, you can't assume that you are somehow immune and disregard an infectious disease as "other people's problem" because the Red Death (or the coronavirus) does not discriminate.<br /><br />Perhaps if Poe wrote this story today, there may be some hint at washing hands regularly, not hoarding hand sanitizers and not fighting over toilet paper.<br /><br /><b>Edit:</b><br />Bumping up the rating to 5 stars in light of recent events. I didn't think this story would have a modern-day equivalent. This is supposedly an allegory of the bubonic plague. Surely, a few centuries later, we would all be better humans who have the common sense to not party during a pandemic right? Except I see news of celebrities who break quarantine to attend parties. I guess celebrities are current day royalties or at least act like that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5270043755865663985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ht1Bxez/g/5zezk7+od4bFecPw24bUdNBY/kC1QYi7Y/k3lLr5n5Q+bPzwMQjukfwJ1KKK+DfOCTpmNDJ12f6g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tTMEA4AT0vAv2r1ubDw3yJkgJ5THTAjDK1i6iTU+26kMfTyNw3WoTbpuS1aGNaa7DiFSsdCiM269cT3BRnvP9Q==')})
  }

However, there is a running Persian thesaurus at the bottom of each page for the more difficult English words highlighted in the text. There are many editions of A.V. Laider. This edition would be useful if you would like to enrich your Persian-English vocabulary, whether for self-improvement or for preparation in advanced of college examinations. Webster\'s edition of this classic is organized to expose the reader to a maximum number of difficult and potentially ambiguous English words. Rare or idiosyncratic words and expressions are given lower priority compared to &quot;difficult, yet commonly used&quot; English words. Rather than supply a single translation, many words are translated for a variety of meanings in Persian, allowing readers to better grasp the ambiguity of English without using the notes as a pure translation crutch We are delighted to publish this classic book as part of our extensive Classic Library collection. Many of the books in our collection have been out of print for decades, and therefore have not been accessible to the general public. The aim of our publishing program is to facilitate rapid access to this vast reservoir of literature, and our view is that this is a significant literary work, which deserves to be brought back into print after many decades. The contents of the vast majority of titles in the Classic Library have been scanned from the original works. To ensure a high quality product, each title has been meticulously hand curated by our staff. Our philosophy has been guided by a desire to provide the reader with a book that is as close as possible to ownership of the original work. Inspiration Lovecraft said the story was inspired by an April 27, 1919 article in<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12712704427839919179\" style=\"display:none\">Beyond the Wall of Sleep&quot; is a short story by American writer H. P. Lovecraft written in 1919 and first published in the amateur publication Pine Cones in October 1919. Inspiration Lovecraft said the story was inspired by an April 27, 1919 article in the New York Tribune. Reporting on the New York state police, the article cited a family named Slater or Slahter as representative of the backwards Catskills population. The nova mentioned at the end of Lovecraft\'s story is a real star, known as GK Persei; the quotation is from Garrett P. Serviss\' Astronomy with the Naked Eye (1908). The title of the story may have been influenced by Ambrose Bierce\'s &quot;Beyond the Wall&quot;; Lovecraft was known to be reading Bierce in 1919. Jack London\'s 1906 novel Before Adam, which concerns the concept of hereditary memory, contains the passage, &quot;Nor...did any of my human kind ever break through the wall of my sleep.<\/span>\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">About Edgar Allan Poe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1454522972p3/4624490.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">Edgar Allan Poe
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
20,395
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9895196208419669009">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of</span>
  <span id="freeText9895196208419669009" style="display:none">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of essays and book reviews. He is widely acknowledged as the inventor of the modern detective story and an innovator in the science fiction genre, but he made his living as America’s first great literary critic and theoretician. Poe’s reputation today rests primarily on his tales of terror as well as on his haunting lyric poetry.<br /><br />Just as the bizarre characters in Poe’s stories have captured the public imagination so too has Poe himself. He is seen as a morbid, mysterious figure lurking in the shadows of moonlit cemeteries or crumbling castles. This is the Poe of legend. But much of what we know about Poe is wrong, the product of a biography written by one of his enemies in an attempt to defame the author’s name.<br /><br />The real Poe was born to traveling actors in Boston on January 19, 1809. Edgar was the second of three children. His other brother William Henry Leonard Poe would also become a poet before his early death, and Poe’s sister Rosalie Poe would grow up to teach penmanship at a Richmond girls’ school. Within three years of Poe’s birth both of his parents had died, and he was taken in by the wealthy tobacco merchant John Allan and his wife Frances Valentine Allan in Richmond, Virginia while Poe’s siblings went to live with other families. Mr. Allan would rear Poe to be a businessman and a Virginia gentleman, but Poe had dreams of being a writer in emulation of his childhood hero the British poet Lord Byron. Early poetic verses found written in a young Poe’s handwriting on the backs of Allan’s ledger sheets reveal how little interest Poe had in the tobacco business.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_allan_poe">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_al...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9895196208419669009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
